by <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">Post-classical history</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ancient_history" title="Template:Ancient history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Ancient_history" title="Template talk:Ancient history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ancient_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p><p><b>Ancient Greece</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">\xce\x95\xce\xbb\xce\xbb\xce\xac\xcf\x82</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">translit.</a>&#160;</small><i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek-language transliteration">Ellas</i>) was a <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> belonging to a period of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greece" title="History of Greece">Greek history</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a> of the 12th\xe2\x80\x939th centuries BC to the end of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Antiquity">antiquity</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> AD 600). Immediately following this period was the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> era.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas1988_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas1988-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Roughly three centuries after the <a href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Late Bronze Age collapse">Late Bronze Age collapse</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a>, Greek urban <a href="/wiki/Poleis" class="mw-redirect" title="Poleis">poleis</a> began to form in the 8th century BC, ushering in the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic period</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">colonization of the Mediterranean Basin</a>. This was followed by the period of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greece</a>, an era that began with the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a>, lasting from the 5th to 4th centuries BC. Due to the conquests by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic civilization</a> flourished from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> to the western end of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic period</a> came to an end with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_wars_and_battles" title="List of Roman wars and battles">conquests</a> and annexations of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">eastern Mediterranean</a> world by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, which established the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Greece">Roman Greece</a>, and later the province of <a href="/wiki/Achaea_(Roman_province)" title="Achaea (Roman province)">Achaea</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.\n</p><p>Classical <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a>, especially philosophy, had a powerful influence on <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, which carried a version of it to many parts of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. For this reason, Classical Greece is generally considered to be the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/seminal" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:seminal">seminal</a> culture which provided the foundation of modern <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> and is considered the cradle of Western civilization.<sup id="cite_ref-EllynMcGinnis2004_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EllynMcGinnis2004-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FindlingPelle2004_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FindlingPelle2004-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ThompsonMullin_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThompsonMullin-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Classical <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a> gave a lot of importance to knowledge. Science and religion were not separate and getting closer to the truth meant getting closer to the <a href="/wiki/Gods" class="mw-redirect" title="Gods">gods</a>. In this context, they understood the importance of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> as an instrument for obtaining more reliable ("divine") knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek culture">Greek culture</a>, in a few centuries and with a limited population, managed to explore and make progress in many fields of science, mathematics, philosophy and knowledge in general.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Chronology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Archaic_period"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Archaic period</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Classical_Greece"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Classical Greece</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Hellenistic_Greece"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Hellenistic Greece</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Roman_Greece"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Roman Greece</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Geography"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Geography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Regions"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Regions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Colonies"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span society</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Political_structure"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Political structure</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Government_and_law"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Government and law</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Social_structure"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Social structure</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#Slavery"><span class="tocnumber">5.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Slavery</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Economy"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Economy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Warfare"><span class="tocnumber">5.6</span> <span class="toctext">Warfare</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Philosophy"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Literature_and_theatre"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Literature and theatre</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Music_and_dance"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Music and dance</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Science_and_technology"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Science and technology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Art_and_architecture"><span class="tocnumber">6.5</span> <span class="toctext">Art and architecture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Religion_and_mythology"><span class="tocnumber">6.6</span> <span class="toctext">Religion and mythology</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_ancient_Greece" title="Timeline of ancient Greece">Timeline of ancient Greece</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Antiquity">Classical Antiquity</a> in the Mediterranean region is commonly considered to have begun in the 8th century BC<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> (around the time of the earliest recorded poetry of Homer) and ended in the 6th century AD.\n</p><p>Classical Antiquity in Greece was preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr></a> 1200 \xe2\x80\x93 c. 800 BC), <a href="/wiki/Archaeologically" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeologically">archaeologically</a> characterised by the <a href="/wiki/Protogeometric_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Protogeometric art">protogeometric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geometric_art" title="Geometric art">geometric styles</a> of designs on pottery. Following the Dark Ages was the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic Period</a>, beginning around the 8th century BC. The Archaic Period saw early developments in Greek culture and society which formed the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> After the Archaic Period, the Classical Period in Greece is conventionally considered to have lasted from the Persian invasion of Greece in 480 until the death of Alexander the Great in 323.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The period is characterized by a style which was considered by later observers to be exemplary, i.e., "classical", as shown in the <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a>, for instance. Politically, the Classical Period was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Athens</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a> during the 5th century, but displaced by <a href="/wiki/Spartan_hegemony" title="Spartan hegemony">Spartan hegemony</a> during the early 4th century BC, before power shifted to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Thebes_(Boeotia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Thebes (Boeotia)">Thebes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Boeotian_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeotian League">Boeotian League</a> and finally to the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Corinth" title="League of Corinth">League of Corinth</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Macedon" class="mw-redirect" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>. This period saw the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rise_of_Macedon" title="Rise of Macedon">Rise of Macedon</a>.\n</p><p>Following the Classical period was the Hellenistic period (323\xe2\x80\x93146 BC), during which Greek culture and power expanded into the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. This period begins with the death of Alexander and ends with the Roman conquest. <a href="/wiki/Roman_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Greece">Roman Greece</a> is usually considered to be the period between Roman victory over the <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinthians</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Corinth_(146_BC)" title="Battle of Corinth (146 BC)">Battle of Corinth</a> in 146 BC and the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> as the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in AD 330. Finally, <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> refers to the period of <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_Greco-Roman_polytheism" title="Decline of Greco-Roman polytheism">Christianization</a> during the later 4th to early 6th centuries AD, sometimes taken to be complete with the closure of the <a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Plato&#39;s Academy">Academy of Athens</a> by <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> in 529.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_historiographers" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek historiographers">Greek historiographers</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg/200px-Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg/300px-Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg/400px-Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Surviving_Greek_Bronze.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Victorious_Youth" title="Victorious Youth">Victorious Youth</a></i> (c. 310 BC), is a rare, water-preserved <a href="/wiki/Bronze_sculpture" title="Bronze sculpture">bronze sculpture</a> from ancient Greece.</div></div></div>\n<p>The historical period of ancient Greece is unique in world history as the first period attested directly in proper <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a>, while earlier ancient history or <a href="/wiki/Proto-history" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-history">proto-history</a> is known by much more circumstantial evidence, such as annals or king lists, and pragmatic <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> is widely known as the "father of history": his <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a></i> are eponymous of the entire <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">field</a>. Written between the 450s and 420s BC, Herodotus\' work reaches about a century into the past, discussing 6th century historical figures such as <a href="/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius I of Persia">Darius I of Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psamtik_III" title="Psamtik III">Psamtik III</a>, and alluding to some 8th century ones such as <a href="/wiki/Candaules" title="Candaules">Candaules</a>.\n</p><p>Herodotus was succeeded by authors such as <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>. Most of these authors were either Athenian or pro-Athenian, which is why far more is known about the history and politics of Athens than those of many other cities.\nTheir scope is further limited by a focus on political, military and diplomatic history, ignoring economic and social history.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greece" title="History of Greece">History of Greece</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaic_period">Archaic period</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archaic_period_in_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaic period in Greece">Archaic period in Greece</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg/220px-Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg/330px-Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg/440px-Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prothesis_Dipylon_Painter_Louvre_A517.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dipylon Vase of the late Geometric period, or the beginning of the Archaic period, <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr></a> 750 BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 8th century BC, Greece began to emerge from the Dark Ages which followed the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean civilization</a>. Literacy had been lost and <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Mycenaean script</a> forgotten, but the Greeks adopted the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>, modifying it to create the Greek alphabet. Objects with Phoenician writing on them may have been available in Greece from the 9th century BC, but the earliest evidence of Greek writing comes from graffiti on Greek pottery from the mid-8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Greece was divided into many small self-governing communities, a pattern largely dictated by Greek geography: every island, valley and plain is cut off from its neighbors by the sea or mountain ranges.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lelantine_War" title="Lelantine War">Lelantine War</a> (c. 710 \xe2\x80\x93 c. 650 BC) is the earliest documented war of the ancient Greek period. It was fought between the important <i><a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">poleis</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Chalcis" title="Chalcis">Chalcis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eretria" title="Eretria">Eretria</a> over the fertile Lelantine plain of <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Euboea</a>. Both cities seem to have suffered a decline as result of the long war, though Chalcis was the nominal victor.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">mercantile class</a> arose in the first half of the 7th century BC, shown by the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_coinage" title="Ancient Greek coinage">coinage</a> in about 680 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-\xc5\xbdi\xc5\xbeek2011_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc5\xbdi\xc5\xbeek2011-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This seems to have introduced tension to many city-states. The <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocratic</a> regimes which generally governed the <i>poleis</i> were threatened by the new-found wealth of merchants, who in turn desired political power. From 650 BC onwards, the aristocracies had to fight not to be overthrown and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a> <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrants</a>. This word derives from the <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">non-pejorative</a> Greek <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xcf\x84\xcf\x8d\xcf\x81\xce\xb1\xce\xbd\xce\xbd\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span> <i>tyrannos</i>, meaning \'illegitimate ruler\', and was applicable to both good and bad leaders alike.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A growing population and a shortage of land also seem to have created internal strife between the poor and the rich in many city-states. In <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Messenian_Wars_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Messenian Wars (disambiguation)">Messenian Wars</a> resulted in the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Messenia_(ancient_region)" title="Messenia (ancient region)">Messenia</a> and enserfment of the Messenians, beginning in the latter half of the 8th century BC, an act without precedent in ancient Greece. This practice allowed a social revolution to occur.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The subjugated population, thenceforth known as <a href="/wiki/Helots" title="Helots">helots</a>, farmed and labored for Sparta, whilst every Spartan male citizen became a soldier of the <a href="/wiki/Spartan_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Spartan Army">Spartan Army</a> in a permanently militarized state. Even the elite were obliged to live and train as soldiers; this commonality between rich and poor citizens served to defuse the social conflict. These reforms, attributed to <a href="/wiki/Lycurgus_of_Sparta" title="Lycurgus of Sparta">Lycurgus of Sparta</a>, were probably complete by 650 BC.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Archaic_Greece_(English).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Map_of_Archaic_Greece_%28English%29.jpg/300px-Map_of_Archaic_Greece_%28English%29.jpg" width="300" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Map_of_Archaic_Greece_%28English%29.jpg/450px-Map_of_Archaic_Greece_%28English%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Map_of_Archaic_Greece_%28English%29.jpg/600px-Map_of_Archaic_Greece_%28English%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="653" data-file-height="520" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Archaic_Greece_(English).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Political geography of ancient Greece in the Archaic and Classical periods</div></div></div>\n<p>Athens suffered a land and agrarian crisis in the late 7th century BC, again resulting in civil strife. The <a href="/wiki/Archon_of_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="Archon of Athens">Archon</a> (chief magistrate) <a href="/wiki/Draco_(lawgiver)" title="Draco (lawgiver)">Draco</a> made severe reforms to the law code in 621 BC (hence "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Draconian" class="extiw" title="wikt:Draconian">draconian</a>"), but these failed to quell the conflict. Eventually the moderate reforms of <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a> (594 BC), improving the lot of the poor but firmly entrenching the aristocracy in power, gave Athens some stability.\n</p><p>By the 6th century BC several cities had emerged as dominant in Greek affairs: Athens, Sparta, <a href="/wiki/Corinth,_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Corinth, Greece">Corinth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thebes_(Greece)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thebes (Greece)">Thebes</a>. Each of them had brought the surrounding rural areas and smaller towns under their control, and Athens and Corinth had become major maritime and mercantile powers as well.\n</p><p>Rapidly increasing population in the 8th and 7th centuries BC had resulted in emigration of many Greeks to form <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">colonies</a> in <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>), <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> and further afield. The emigration effectively ceased in the 6th century BC by which time the Greek world had, culturally and linguistically, become much larger than the area of present-day Greece. Greek colonies were not politically controlled by their founding cities, although they often retained religious and commercial links with them.\n</p><p>The emigration process also determined a long series of conflicts between the Greek cities of Sicily, especially <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily#Greek_period" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Carthaginians</a>. These conflicts lasted from 600 BC to 265 BC when the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> entered into an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Mamertines" title="Mamertines">Mamertines</a> to fend off the hostilities by the new tyrant of Syracuse, <a href="/wiki/Hiero_II_of_Syracuse" title="Hiero II of Syracuse">Hiero II</a> and then the Carthaginians. This way Rome became the new dominant power against the fading strength of the Sicilian Greek cities and the Carthaginian supremacy in the region. One year later the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a> erupted.\n</p><p>In this period, there was huge economic development in Greece, and also in its overseas colonies which experienced a growth in commerce and manufacturing. There was a great improvement in the living standards of the population. Some studies estimate that the average size of the Greek household, in the period from 800 BC to 300 BC, increased five times, which indicates<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> a large increase in the average income of the population.\n</p><p>In the second half of the 6th century BC, Athens fell under the tyranny of <a href="/wiki/Peisistratos_(Athens)" class="mw-redirect" title="Peisistratos (Athens)">Peisistratos</a> and then of his sons <a href="/wiki/Hippias_(tyrant)" title="Hippias (tyrant)">Hippias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hipparchos_(son_of_Peisistratos)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hipparchos (son of Peisistratos)">Hipparchos</a>. However, in 510 BC, at the instigation of the Athenian aristocrat <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes" title="Cleisthenes">Cleisthenes</a>, the Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Cleomenes_I" title="Cleomenes I">Cleomenes I</a> helped the Athenians overthrow the tyranny. Afterwards, Sparta and Athens promptly turned on each other, at which point Cleomenes I installed <a href="/wiki/Isagoras" title="Isagoras">Isagoras</a> as a pro-Spartan archon. Eager to prevent Athens from becoming a Spartan puppet, Cleisthenes responded by proposing to his fellow citizens that Athens undergo a revolution: that all citizens share in political power, regardless of status: that Athens become a "<a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">democracy</a>". So enthusiastically did the Athenians take to this idea that, having overthrown Isagoras and implemented Cleisthenes\'s reforms, they were easily able to repel a Spartan-led three-pronged invasion aimed at restoring Isagoras.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The advent of the democracy cured many of the ills of Athens and led to a \'<a href="/wiki/Fifth-century_Athens" title="Fifth-century Athens">golden age</a>\' for the Athenians.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Greece">Classical Greece</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greece</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg/220px-EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg/330px-EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg/440px-EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1213" data-file-height="609" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EarlyAthenianCoin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athenian</a> coin, depicting the head of <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> on the obverse and her owl on the reverse\xe2\x80\x945th century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>In 499 BC, the Ionian city states under Persian rule rebelled against the Persian-supported tyrants that ruled them.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Supported by troops sent from Athens and <a href="/wiki/Eretria" title="Eretria">Eretria</a>, they advanced as far as <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> and burnt the city down, before being driven back by a Persian counterattack.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The revolt continued until 494, when the rebelling Ionians were defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Darius did not forget that the Athenians had assisted the Ionian revolt, however, and in 490 he assembled an armada to conquer Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Despite being heavily outnumbered, the Athenians \xe2\x80\x93 supported by their <a href="/wiki/Plataea" title="Plataea">Plataean</a> allies \xe2\x80\x93 defeated the Persian forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a>, and the Persian fleet withdrew.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/300px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png" width="300" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/450px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/600px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="992" data-file-height="793" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing events of the first phases of the Greco-Persian Wars.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg/300px-Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg.png" width="300" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg/450px-Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg/600px-Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="993" data-file-height="794" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Delian League ("Athenian Empire"), immediately before the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a> in 431 BC</div></div></div>\n<p>Ten years later, a <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">second invasion</a> was launched by Darius\' son <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The city-states of northern and central Greece submitted to the Persian forces without resistance, but a coalition of 31 Greek city states, including Athens and Sparta, determined to resist the Persian invaders.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, Greek Sicily was invaded by a Carthaginian force.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In 480 BC, the first major battle of the invasion was fought at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Thermopylae</a>, where a small force of Greeks, led by three hundred Spartans, held a crucial pass into the heart of Greece for several days; at the same time <a href="/wiki/Gelo" title="Gelo">Gelon</a>, tyrant of Syracuse, defeated the Carthaginian invasion at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Himera_(480_BC)" title="Battle of Himera (480 BC)">Battle of Himera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Persians were defeated by a primarily Athenian naval force at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>, and in 479 defeated on land at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The alliance against Persia continued, initially led by the Spartan <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(general)" title="Pausanias (general)">Pausanias</a> but from 477 by Athens,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and by 460 Persia had been driven out of the Aegean.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> During this period of campaigning, the <a href="/wiki/Delian_league" class="mw-redirect" title="Delian league">Delian league</a> gradually transformed from a defensive alliance of Greek states into an Athenian empire, as Athens\' growing naval power enabled it to compel other league states to comply with its policies.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Athens ended its campaigns against Persia in 450 BC, after a disastrous defeat in Egypt in 454 BC, and the death of <a href="/wiki/Cimon" title="Cimon">Cimon</a> in action against the Persians on Cyprus in 450.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Athenian activity against the Persian empire was ending, however, conflict between Sparta and Athens was increasing. Sparta was suspicious of the increasing Athenian power funded by the Delian League, and tensions rose when Sparta offered aid to reluctant members of the League to rebel against Athenian domination. These tensions were exacerbated in 462, when Athens sent a force to aid Sparta in overcoming a <a href="/wiki/Helot" class="mw-redirect" title="Helot">helot</a> revolt, but their aid was rejected by the Spartans.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In the 450s, Athens took control of Boeotia, and won victories over <a href="/wiki/Aegina" title="Aegina">Aegina</a> and Corinth.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> However, Athens failed to win a decisive victory, and in 447 lost Boeotia again.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Athens and Sparta signed the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_Peace" title="Thirty Years&#39; Peace">Thirty Years\' Peace</a> in the winter of 446/5, ending the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the peace of 446/5, Athenian relations with Sparta declined again in the 430s, and in 431 war broke out once again.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Archidamian_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Archidamian War">first phase of the war</a> is traditionally seen as a series of annual invasions of Attica by Sparta, which made little progress, while Athens were successful against the Corinthian empire in the north-west of Greece, and in defending their own empire, despite suffering from <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Athens" title="Plague of Athens">plague</a> and Spartan invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The turning point of this phase of the war usually seen as the Athenian victories at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pylos" title="Battle of Pylos">Pylos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sphacteria" title="Battle of Sphacteria">Sphakteria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Sparta sued for peace, but the Athenians rejected the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The Athenian failure to regain control at Boeotia at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Delium" title="Battle of Delium">Delium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brasidas" title="Brasidas">Brasidas</a>\' successes in the north of Greece in 424, improved Sparta\'s position after Sphakteria.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> After the deaths of Cleon and Brasidas, the strongest objectors to peace on the Athenian and Spartan sides respectively, <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Nikias" class="mw-redirect" title="Peace of Nikias">a peace treaty</a> was agreed in 421.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The peace did not last, however. In 418 an alliance between Athens and Argos was defeated by Sparta at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mantinea_(418_BC)" title="Battle of Mantinea (418 BC)">Mantinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In 415 Athens launched a naval expedition against Sicily;<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> the expedition ended in disaster with almost the entire army killed.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Soon after the Athenian defeat in Syracuse, Athens\' Ionian allies began to rebel against the Delian league, while at the same time Persia began to once again involve itself in Greek affairs on the Spartan side.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Initially the Athenian position continued to be relatively strong, winning important battles such as those at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cyzicus" title="Battle of Cyzicus">Cyzicus</a> in 410 and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arginusae" title="Battle of Arginusae">Arginusae</a> in 406.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> However, in 405 the Spartans defeated Athens in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aegospotami" title="Battle of Aegospotami">Battle of Aegospotami</a>, and began to blockade Athens\' harbour;<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> with no grain supply and in danger of starvation, Athens sued for peace, agreeing to surrender their fleet and join the Spartan-led Peloponnesian League.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Greece thus entered the 4th century BC under a Spartan hegemony, but it was clear from the start that this was weak. A demographic crisis meant Sparta was overstretched, and by 395 BC Athens, Argos, Thebes, and Corinth felt able to challenge Spartan dominance, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_War" title="Corinthian War">Corinthian War</a> (395\xe2\x80\x93387 BC). Another war of stalemates, it ended with the <a href="/wiki/Status_quo" title="Status quo">status quo</a> restored, after the threat of Persian intervention on behalf of the Spartans.\n</p><p>The Spartan hegemony lasted another 16 years, until, when attempting to impose their will on the Thebans, the Spartans were defeated at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leuctra" title="Battle of Leuctra">Leuctra</a> in 371 BC. The Theban general <a href="/wiki/Epaminondas" title="Epaminondas">Epaminondas</a> then led Theban troops into the Peloponnese, whereupon other city-states defected from the Spartan cause. The Thebans were thus able to march into Messenia and free the population.\n</p><p>Deprived of land and its serfs, Sparta declined to a second-rank power. The <a href="/wiki/Theban_hegemony" title="Theban hegemony">Theban hegemony</a> thus established was short-lived; at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mantinea_(362_BC)" title="Battle of Mantinea (362 BC)">Battle of Mantinea</a> in 362 BC, Thebes lost its key leader, Epaminondas, and much of its manpower, even though they were victorious in battle. In fact such were the losses to all the great city-states at Mantinea that none could establish dominance in the aftermath.\n</p><p>The weakened state of the heartland of Greece coincided with the Rise of Macedon, led by <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a>. In twenty years, Philip had unified his kingdom, expanded it north and west at the expense of <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrian tribes</a>, and then conquered <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> and Thrace. His success stemmed from his innovative reforms to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_army" title="Ancient Macedonian army">Macedonian army</a>. Phillip intervened repeatedly in the affairs of the southern city-states, culminating in his invasion of 338 BC.\n</p><p>Decisively defeating an allied army of Thebes and Athens at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaeronea_(338_BC)" title="Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)">Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)</a>, he became <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemon</a> of all of Greece, except Sparta. He compelled the majority of the city-states to join the League of Corinth, allying them to him, and preventing them from warring with each other. Philip then entered into war against the Achaemenid Empire but was assassinated by <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_of_Orestis" title="Pausanias of Orestis">Pausanias of Orestis</a> early on in the conflict.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, son and successor of Philip, continued the war. Alexander defeated <a href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III of Persia</a> and completely destroyed the Achaemenid Empire, annexing it to Macedon and earning himself the epithet \'the Great\'. When Alexander died in 323 BC, Greek power and influence was at its zenith. However, there had been a fundamental shift away from the fierce independence and classical culture of the <i>poleis</i>\xe2\x80\x94and instead towards the developing <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic culture</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_Greece">Hellenistic Greece</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">Wars of Alexander the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg/220px-Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg/330px-Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg/440px-Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander Mosaic, National Archaeological Museum, Naples.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Hellenistic period lasted from 323 BC, which marked the end of the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">wars of Alexander the Great</a>, to the annexation of Greece by the Roman Republic in 146 BC. Although the establishment of Roman rule did not break the continuity of Hellenistic society and culture, which remained essentially unchanged until the advent of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, it did mark the end of Greek political independence.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/300px-Diadochen1.png" width="300" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/450px-Diadochen1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/600px-Diadochen1.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="961" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The major <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> realms included the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi kingdoms</a>: <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #787CAD; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy I Soter</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #50A249; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #C38833; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #C3B933; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #AF3662; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a></div> Also shown on the map: <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #85AB54; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Greek_colonies" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek colonies">Greek colonies</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #A361BD; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> (non-Greek)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #70A9BE; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a> (non-Greek)</div>  The orange areas were often in dispute after 281 BCE. The <a href="/wiki/Attalid_dynasty" title="Attalid dynasty">Attalid dynasty</a> occupied some of this area. Not shown: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the death of Alexander, his empire was, after quite some conflict, divided among his generals, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> (<a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and adjoining <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Antigonid_dynasty" title="Antigonid dynasty">Antigonid dynasty</a> (Macedonia). In the intervening period, the poleis of Greece were able to wrest back some of their freedom, although still nominally subject to the Macedonian Kingdom.\n</p><p>During the Hellenistic period, the importance of "Greece proper" (that is, the territory of modern Greece) within the Greek-speaking world declined sharply. The great centers of Hellenistic culture were <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, capitals of the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, respectively.\n</p><p>The conquests of Alexander had numerous consequences for the Greek city-states. It greatly widened the horizons of the Greeks and led to a steady emigration, particularly of the young and ambitious, to the new Greek empires in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Many Greeks migrated to Alexandria, Antioch and the many other new Hellenistic cities founded in Alexander\'s wake, as far away as what are now <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a> survived until the end of the first century BC.\n</p><p>The city-states within Greece formed themselves into two leagues; the <a href="/wiki/Achaean_League" title="Achaean League">Achaean League</a> (including Thebes, Corinth and Argos) and the <a href="/wiki/Aetolian_League" title="Aetolian League">Aetolian League</a> (including Sparta and Athens). For much of the period until the Roman conquest, these leagues were usually at war with each other, and/or allied to different sides in the conflicts between the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a> (the successor states to Alexander\'s empire).\n</p><p>The Antigonid Kingdom became involved in a war with the Roman Republic in the late 3rd century. Although the <a href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_War" title="First Macedonian War">First Macedonian War</a> was inconclusive, the Romans, in typical fashion, continued to make war on Macedon until it was completely absorbed into the Roman Republic (by 149 BC). In the east the unwieldy Seleucid Empire gradually disintegrated, although a rump survived until 64 BC, whilst the Ptolemaic Kingdom continued in Egypt until 30 BC, when it too was conquered by the Romans. The Aetolian league grew wary of Roman involvement in Greece, and sided with the Seleucids in the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Seleucid_War" title="Roman\xe2\x80\x93Seleucid War">Roman\xe2\x80\x93Seleucid War</a>; when the Romans were victorious, the league was effectively absorbed into the Republic. Although the Achaean league outlasted both the Aetolian league and Macedon, it was also soon defeated and absorbed by the Romans in 146 BC, bringing an end to the independence of all of Greece.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Greece">Roman Greece</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Greece">Roman Greece</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greece" title="Byzantine Greece">Byzantine Greece</a></div>\n<p>The Greek peninsula came under Roman rule during the 146 BC conquest of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> after the Battle of Corinth. <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a> became a <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> while southern Greece came under the surveillance of Macedonia\'s <a href="/wiki/Prefect" title="Prefect">prefect</a>; however, some Greek poleis managed to maintain a partial independence and avoid taxation. The <a href="/wiki/Aegean_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Aegean islands">Aegean islands</a> were added to this territory in 133 BC. <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and other Greek cities revolted in 88 BC, and the peninsula was crushed by the Roman general <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Sulla</a>. The Roman civil wars devastated the land even further, until <a href="/wiki/Caesar_Augustus" class="mw-redirect" title="Caesar Augustus">Augustus</a> organized the peninsula as the province of <a href="/wiki/Achaea_(province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Achaea (province)">Achaea</a> in 27 BC.\n</p><p>Greece was a key eastern province of the Roman Empire, as the <a href="/wiki/Roman_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman culture">Roman culture</a> had long been in fact <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek language</a> served as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/East" title="East">East</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and many Greek intellectuals such as <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> would perform most of their work in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions">Regions</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_ancient_Greece" title="Regions of ancient Greece">Regions of ancient Greece</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png/280px-Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png" width="280" height="324" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png/420px-Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png/560px-Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png 2x" data-file-width="950" data-file-height="1100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the major regions of mainland ancient Greece and adjacent "barbarian" lands.</div></div></div>\n<p>The territory of Greece is mountainous, and as a result, ancient Greece consisted of many smaller regions each with its own dialect, cultural peculiarities, and identity. Regionalism and regional conflicts were a prominent feature of ancient Greece. Cities tended to be located in valleys between mountains, or on coastal plains, and dominated a certain area around them.\n</p><p>In the south lay the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, itself consisting of the regions of Laconia (southeast), Messenia (southwest), Elis (west), Achaia (north), Korinthia (northeast), Argolis (east), and Arcadia (center). These names survive to the present day as <a href="/wiki/Regional_units_of_Greece" title="Regional units of Greece">regional units of modern Greece</a>, though with somewhat different boundaries. Mainland Greece to the north, nowadays known as <a href="/wiki/Central_Greece" title="Central Greece">Central Greece</a>, consisted of <a href="/wiki/Aetolia" title="Aetolia">Aetolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acarnania" title="Acarnania">Acarnania</a> in the west, <a href="/wiki/Locris" title="Locris">Locris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doris_(Greece)" title="Doris (Greece)">Doris</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phocis_(ancient_region)" title="Phocis (ancient region)">Phocis</a> in the center, while in the east lay <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Megaris" title="Megaris">Megaris</a>. Northeast lay <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> lay to the northwest. Epirus stretched from the <a href="/wiki/Ambracian_Gulf" title="Ambracian Gulf">Ambracian Gulf</a> in the south to the <a href="/wiki/Ceraunian_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceraunian mountains">Ceraunian mountains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aoos" class="mw-redirect" title="Aoos">Aoos</a> river in the north, and consisted of <a href="/wiki/Chaonia" title="Chaonia">Chaonia</a> (north), <a href="/wiki/Molossia" class="mw-redirect" title="Molossia">Molossia</a> (center), and Thesprotia (south). In the northeast corner was Macedonia,<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> originally consisting <a href="/wiki/Lower_Macedonia" title="Lower Macedonia">Lower Macedonia</a> and its regions, such as <a href="/wiki/Elimeia" title="Elimeia">Elimeia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pieria_(regional_unit)" title="Pieria (regional unit)">Pieria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orestis_(region)" title="Orestis (region)">Orestis</a>. Around the time of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Macedon" title="Alexander I of Macedon">Alexander I of Macedon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Argead_dynasty" title="Argead dynasty">Argead kings of Macedon</a> started to expand into <a href="/wiki/Upper_Macedonia" title="Upper Macedonia">Upper Macedonia</a>, lands inhabited by independent Macedonian tribes like the <a href="/wiki/Lynkestis" title="Lynkestis">Lyncestae</a> and the Elmiotae and to the West, beyond the <a href="/wiki/Axius_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Axius river">Axius river</a>, into <a href="/wiki/Eordaia" title="Eordaia">Eordaia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bottiaea" title="Bottiaea">Bottiaea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mygdonia" title="Mygdonia">Mygdonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Almopia" title="Almopia">Almopia</a>, regions settled by Thracian tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> To the north of Macedonia lay various non-Greek peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Paeonians" class="mw-redirect" title="Paeonians">Paeonians</a> due north, the <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracians</a> to the northeast, and the <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrians</a>, with whom the Macedonians were frequently in conflict, to the northwest. <a href="/wiki/Chalcidice" class="mw-redirect" title="Chalcidice">Chalcidice</a> was settled early on by southern Greek colonists and was considered part of the Greek world, while from the late 2nd millennium BC substantial Greek settlement also occurred on the eastern shores of the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonies">Colonies</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_pre-Roman_Gaul" title="Greeks in pre-Roman Gaul">Greeks in pre-Roman Gaul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_cities_in_Thrace_and_Dacia#Greek" title="List of ancient cities in Thrace and Dacia">List of ancient cities in Thrace and Dacia \xc2\xa7&#160;Greek</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/280px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" width="280" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/420px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/560px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2836" data-file-height="1616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek cities &amp; colonies <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr></a> 550 BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the Archaic period, the <a href="/wiki/Classical_demography#Ancient_Greece_and_Greek_colonies" title="Classical demography">population of Greece</a> grew beyond the capacity of its limited <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> (according to one estimate, the population of ancient Greece increased by a factor larger than ten during the period from 800 BC to 400 BC, increasing from a population of 800,000 to a total estimated population of 10 to 13 million).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From about 750 BC the Greeks began 250 years of expansion, settling colonies in all directions. To the east, the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> coast of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> was colonized first, followed by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history_of_Cyprus" title="Ancient history of Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a> and south coast of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.\n</p><p>Eventually Greek colonization reached as far northeast as present day <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and Russia (<a href="/wiki/Taganrog" title="Taganrog">Taganrog</a>). To the west the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and Southern Italy were settled, followed by <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">Southern France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, and even northeastern <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. Greek colonies were also founded in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libya</a>.\n</p><p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Syracuse, Italy">Syracuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> and <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> had their beginnings as the Greek colonies Syracusae <i>(\xce\xa3\xcf\x85\xcf\x81\xce\xb1\xce\xba\xce\xbf\xcf\x8d\xcf\x83\xce\xb1\xce\xb9)</i>, Neapolis <i>(\xce\x9d\xce\xb5\xce\xac\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xce\xbb\xce\xb9\xcf\x82)</i>, Massalia <i>(\xce\x9c\xce\xb1\xcf\x83\xcf\x83\xce\xb1\xce\xbb\xce\xaf\xce\xb1)</i> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantion</a> <i>(\xce\x92\xcf\x85\xce\xb6\xce\xac\xce\xbd\xcf\x84\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xce\xbd)</i>. These colonies played an important role in the spread of Greek influence throughout Europe and also aided in the establishment of long-distance trading networks between the Greek city-states, boosting the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_ancient_Greece" title="Economy of ancient Greece">economy of ancient Greece</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_society">Politics and society</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_structure">Political structure</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_citizenship#Ancient_Greece" title="History of citizenship">History of citizenship \xc2\xa7&#160;Ancient Greece</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg/220px-Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg/330px-Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg/440px-Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1700" data-file-height="2570" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bust_Pericles_Chiaramonti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marble bust of Pericles with a <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_helmet" title="Corinthian helmet">Corinthian helmet</a>, Roman copy of a Greek original, <a href="/wiki/Museo_Chiaramonti" class="mw-redirect" title="Museo Chiaramonti">Museo Chiaramonti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Museums" title="Vatican Museums">Vatican Museums</a>; Pericles was a key <a href="/wiki/Populist" class="mw-redirect" title="Populist">populist</a> political figure in the development of the radical <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">Athenian democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Ancient Greece consisted of several hundred relatively independent <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Poleis" class="mw-redirect" title="Poleis">poleis</a></i>). This was a situation unlike that in most other contemporary societies, which were either <a href="/wiki/Tribe" title="Tribe">tribal</a> or kingdoms ruling over relatively large territories. Undoubtedly the <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Greece" title="Geography of Greece">geography of Greece</a>\xe2\x80\x94divided and sub-divided by hills, mountains, and rivers\xe2\x80\x94contributed to the fragmentary nature of ancient Greece. On the one hand, the ancient Greeks had no doubt that they were "one people"; they had the same religion, same basic culture, and same language. Furthermore, the Greeks were very aware of their tribal origins; Herodotus was able to extensively categorise the city-states by tribe. Yet, although these higher-level relationships existed, they seem to have rarely had a major role in Greek politics. The independence of the <i>poleis</i> was fiercely defended; unification was something rarely contemplated by the ancient Greeks. Even when, during the second Persian invasion of Greece, a group of city-states allied themselves to defend Greece, the vast majority of <i>poleis</i> remained neutral, and after the Persian defeat, the allies quickly returned to infighting.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thus, the major peculiarities of the ancient Greek political system were firstly, its fragmentary nature, and that this does not particularly seem to have tribal origin, and secondly, the particular focus on urban centers within otherwise tiny states. The peculiarities of the Greek system are further evidenced by the colonies that they set up throughout the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, which, though they might count a certain Greek <i>polis</i> as their \'mother\' (and remain sympathetic to her), were completely independent of the founding city.\n</p><p>Inevitably smaller <i>poleis</i> might be dominated by larger neighbors, but conquest or direct rule by another city-state appears to have been quite rare. Instead the <i>poleis</i> grouped themselves into leagues, membership of which was in a constant state of flux. Later in the Classical period, the leagues would become fewer and larger, be dominated by one city (particularly Athens, Sparta and Thebes); and often <i>poleis</i> would be compelled to join under threat of war (or as part of a peace treaty). Even after Philip II of Macedon "conquered" the heartlands of ancient Greece, he did not attempt to annex the territory, or unify it into a new province, but simply compelled most of the <i>poleis</i> to join his own <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Corinthian League">Corinthian League</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_law">Government and law</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_law" title="Ancient Greek law">Ancient Greek law</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg/220px-Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg/330px-Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg/440px-Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1730" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Law_Code_Gortyn_Louvre_Ma703.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inheritance law, part of the <a href="/wiki/Gortyn_code" title="Gortyn code">Law Code of Gortyn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, fragment of the 11th column. Limestone, 5th century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>Initially many Greek city-states seem to have been petty kingdoms; there was often a city official carrying some residual, ceremonial functions of the king (<i><a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">basileus</a></i>), e.g., the <i><a href="/wiki/Archon_basileus" title="Archon basileus">archon basileus</a></i> in Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> However, by the Archaic period and the first historical consciousness, most had already become aristocratic <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchies</a>. It is unclear exactly how this change occurred. For instance, in Athens, the kingship had been reduced to a hereditary, lifelong chief magistracy (<i><a href="/wiki/Archon_of_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="Archon of Athens">archon</a></i>) by <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr></a> 1050 BC; by 753 BC this had become a decennial, elected archonship; and finally by 683 BC an annually elected archonship. Through each stage more power would have been transferred to the aristocracy as a whole, and away from a single individual.\n</p><p>Inevitably, the domination of politics and concomitant aggregation of wealth by small groups of families was apt to cause social unrest in many <i>poleis</i>. In many cities a <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrant</a> (not in the modern sense of repressive autocracies), would at some point seize control and govern according to their own will; often a populist agenda would help sustain them in power. In a system wracked with <a href="/wiki/Class_conflict" title="Class conflict">class conflict</a>, government by a \'strongman\' was often the best solution.\n</p><p>Athens fell under a tyranny in the second half of the 6th century. When this tyranny was ended, the Athenians founded the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/History_of_democracy" title="History of democracy">democracy</a> as a radical solution to prevent the aristocracy regaining power. A <a href="/wiki/Popular_assembly" title="Popular assembly">citizens\' assembly</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(ancient_Athens)" title="Ecclesia (ancient Athens)">Ecclesia</a></i>), for the discussion of city policy, had existed since the reforms of <a href="/wiki/Draco_(lawgiver)" title="Draco (lawgiver)">Draco</a> in 621 BC; all citizens were permitted to attend after the reforms of <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a> (early 6th century), but the poorest citizens could not address the assembly or run for office. With the establishment of the democracy, the assembly became the <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> mechanism of government; all citizens had equal privileges in the assembly. However, non-citizens, such as <a href="/wiki/Metic" title="Metic">metics</a> (foreigners living in Athens) or <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Greece" title="Slavery in ancient Greece">slaves</a>, had no political rights at all.\n</p><p>After the rise of the democracy in Athens, other city-states founded democracies. However, many retained more traditional forms of government. As so often in other matters, Sparta was a notable exception to the rest of Greece, ruled through the whole period by not one, but two hereditary monarchs. This was a form of <a href="/wiki/Diarchy" title="Diarchy">diarchy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Sparta" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of Sparta">Kings of Sparta</a> belonged to the Agiads and the Eurypontids, descendants respectively of <a href="/wiki/Eurysthenes" title="Eurysthenes">Eurysthenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Procles" title="Procles">Procles</a>. Both dynasties\' founders were believed to be twin sons of <a href="/wiki/Aristodemus" title="Aristodemus">Aristodemus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Heracleidae" title="Heracleidae">Heraclid</a> ruler.
